# Problem 2867

- Domain: `coding`

## Problem

```text
You are given a binary array nums.
A subarray of an array is good if it contains exactly one element with the value 1.
Return an integer denoting the number of ways to split the array nums into good subarrays. As the number may be too large, return it modulo 10^9 + 7.
A subarray is a contiguous non-empty sequence of elements within an array.
 
Example 1:

Input: nums = [0,1,0,0,1]
Output: 3
Explanation: There are 3 ways to split nums into good subarrays:
- [0,1] [0,0,1]
- [0,1,0] [0,1]
- [0,1,0,0] [1]

Example 2:

Input: nums = [0,1,0]
Output: 1
Explanation: There is 1 way to split nums into good subarrays:
- [0,1,0]

 
Constraints:

1 <= nums.length <= 10^5
0 <= nums[i] <= 1
```

### Starter Code

```python
class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
```

## Generation

### System Prompt

```text
You are an expert Python programmer. You will be given a question (problem specification) and will generate a correct Python program that matches the specification and passes all tests.
```

### User Prompt

```text
### Question:
You are given a binary array nums.
A subarray of an array is good if it contains exactly one element with the value 1.
Return an integer denoting the number of ways to split the array nums into good subarrays. As the number may be too large, return it modulo 10^9 + 7.
A subarray is a contiguous non-empty sequence of elements within an array.
 
Example 1:

Input: nums = [0,1,0,0,1]
Output: 3
Explanation: There are 3 ways to split nums into good subarrays:
- [0,1] [0,0,1]
- [0,1,0] [0,1]
- [0,1,0,0] [1]

Example 2:

Input: nums = [0,1,0]
Output: 1
Explanation: There is 1 way to split nums into good subarrays:
- [0,1,0]

 
Constraints:

1 <= nums.length <= 10^5
0 <= nums[i] <= 1

### Format: You will use the following starter code to write the solution to the problem and enclose your code within delimiters.
```python
class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        
```

### Answer: (use the provided format with backticks)
```

### Outputs

<details>
<summary>Generation 1</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        positions = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(positions)

        # If there are no ones, it's impossible to have any good subarray (requires exactly one 1).
        if k == 0:
            return 0

        # Each good subarray must contain exactly one 1, so we must split into k parts.
        # For consecutive ones at positions a and b, let gap = b - a - 1 (zeros between them).
        # We must place some suffix of those zeros with the left subarray and the rest with the right.
        # That gives (gap + 1) choices per adjacent pair of ones.
        ans = 1
        for i in range(k - 1):
            gap = positions[i + 1] - positions[i] - 1
            ans = (ans * (gap + 1)) % MOD

        # Zeros before the first 1 and after the last 1 are forced to belong to their respective nearest 1 subarray.
        return ans
```
```

</details>

<summary>Generation 2</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)
        if k == 0:
            return 0  # cannot form good subarrays (each good subarray must contain exactly one 1)

        # dp over groups of ones:
        # Split positions can be placed only in zeros between consecutive ones.
        # For each gap (one-to-one), if there are z zeros between them, then each split
        # boundary inside that gap has (z choices). Additionally, all zeros can be distributed
        # in a way that guarantees the adjacent ones end up in different subarrays.
        # In fact, number of valid split ways equals product over gaps of (z + 1),
        # where z is number of zeros between consecutive ones.
        #
        # Zeros at the ends (before first 1 and after last 1) are forced into the only
        # subarrays that contain those boundary ones.
        ans = 1
        for i in range(1, k):
            left = ones[i - 1]
            right = ones[i]
            z = right - left - 1  # zeros between these two ones
            ans = (ans * (z + 1)) % MOD

        return ans
```
```

</details>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        # Collect indices of all ones
        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)

        # If no ones, impossible because each good subarray must contain exactly one '1'
        if k == 0:
            return 0

        # If exactly one 1, the whole array must be taken as the single good subarray
        if k == 1:
            return 1

        # For consecutive ones at positions p=ones[i], q=ones[i+1],
        # we can choose where the split boundary falls inside the gap [p+1, q-1].
        # That count equals number of zeros between them, plus 1.
        ans = 1
        for i in range(k - 1):
            gap_zeros = ones[i + 1] - ones[i] - 1
            ans = (ans * (gap_zeros + 1)) % MOD

        return ans
```

```python
from typing import List

MOD = 10**9 + 7

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)
        if k == 0:
            return 0
        if k == 1:
            # Whole array is the only good subarray split
            return 1

        ans = 1
        # For every gap between consecutive ones: indices (ones[j], ones[j+1])
        # Let d be the number of zeros between them.
        # We need choose a cut position after the earlier one within the zeros,
        # ensuring each segment contains exactly one '1'.
        # Number of choices = zeros_between + 1 = ones[j+1] - ones[j]
        for j in range(k - 1):
            zeros_between = ones[j+1] - ones[j] - 1
            ans = (ans * (zeros_between + 1)) % MOD
        return ans
```

from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7
        n = len(nums)

        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)

        # Every good subarray must contain exactly one '1',
        # so if there are 0 or more than 0 ones constraints make impossible.
        if k == 0:
            return 0  # cannot form good subarray (non-empty) with exactly one 1

        # If we split into k good subarrays, between each consecutive pair of ones,
        # we may place the boundary somewhere in the zeros-run between them.
        ans = 1
        for j in range(k - 1):
            gap = ones[j + 1] - ones[j] - 1  # number of zeros between two ones
            ans = (ans * (gap + 1)) % MOD

        return ans
```

```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        # Total count of ways:
        # Let positions of ones be at indices p0 < p1 < ... < p{k-1}.
        # Each segment must contain exactly one '1'.
        # Zeros after the first one and before the next one must be distributed
        # between neighboring segments: if there are (d) zeros between ones,
        # there are (d+1) ways for the boundary.
        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)
        if k == 0:
            return 0  # cannot split into non-empty good subarrays

        ans = 1
        for i in range(k - 1):
            zeros_between = ones[i + 1] - ones[i] - 1
            ans = (ans * (zeros_between + 1)) % MOD
        return ans
```

<summary>Generation 7</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        # Collect indices of all 1s
        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)

        # If no 1 or only one 1: whole array must be the only good subarray
        # (subarray must contain exactly one '1' and must cover the array).
        if k <= 1:
            return 1

        # For two consecutive 1s at positions ones[j-1], ones[j],
        # the subarray containing ones[j-1] ends somewhere in (ones[j-1], ones[j])
        # but must not include ones[j]. So choices are the number of zeros between
        # them plus 1 (cut positions between them).
        ans = 1
        for j in range(1, k):
            gap = ones[j] - ones[j - 1]  # distance between 1s
            # Between them there are gap-1 elements, all zeros.
            # Number of cut choices equals (gap) because start after previous cut,
            # equivalently choose end position for left subarray among those gap elements.
            ans = (ans * gap) % MOD

        return ans
```
```

</details>

<details>
<summary>Generation 8</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

MOD = 10**9 + 7

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        # Find positions of all 1s
        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)
        
        # If there are 0 ones, it's impossible to have any good subarray
        # (but constraints don't specify; still handle safely)
        if k == 0:
            return 0
        
        # If there's only one 1, the whole array must be exactly one good subarray
        # because any split would create a subarray without exactly one '1'.
        if k == 1:
            return 1
        
        # Between consecutive 1s, count zeros separating them.
        # Let zeros[i] be number of zeros between ones[i] and ones[i+1].
        ways = 1
        for i in range(k - 1):
            gap = ones[i + 1] - ones[i] - 1  # number of zeros between these ones
            # We must decide where to cut: after the i-th 1's subarray,
            # so choose an index among gap zeros plus 1 cut options.
            ways = (ways * (gap + 1)) % MOD
        
        return ways
```
```

</details>

<details>
<summary>Generation 9</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        # Positions of ones
        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)

        # If no 1, can't make any non-empty subarray with exactly one 1
        if k == 0:
            return 0

        # If exactly one 1, the whole array must be the single good subarray
        # (and it will contain exactly that one 1). Only 1 split.
        if k == 1:
            return 1

        # Let gaps[i] be the number of zeros between ones[i] and ones[i+1]
        # For each gap, we distribute some trailing zeros of the left part
        # to the left subarray so that the right subarray still contains exactly
        # one 1. That gives (gap + 1) choices per internal boundary.
        ans = 1
        for i in range(k - 1):
            gap_zeros = ones[i + 1] - ones[i] - 1
            ans = (ans * (gap_zeros + 1)) % MOD

        return ans
```
```

</details>

<details>
<summary>Generation 10</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        # Count ones and positions of ones
        ones = [i for i, v in enumerate(nums) if v == 1]
        k = len(ones)
        if k == 0:
            return 0  # cannot make any good subarray (needs exactly one '1' each)

        # For each gap of zeros between consecutive ones, compute choices:
        # Suppose between ones[i] and ones[i+1] there are z zeros.
        # Split boundary must be placed at one of these z+1 cut positions
        # to ensure each side has exactly one 1.
        ans = 1
        for i in range(k - 1):
            left_one = ones[i]
            right_one = ones[i + 1]
            z = right_one - left_one - 1  # number of zeros between the two ones
            ans = (ans * (z + 1)) % MOD

        # Zeros before the first 1 and after the last 1 are forced:
        # left part must end at first 1, right part must start at last 1.
        return ans
```
```

</details>

## Memory

- Skipped: `false`

### Prompt

```text
## Guidelines
You need to extract and summarize useful insights in the format of memory items based on comparing the successful and unsuccessful solution attempts below.
The goal of summarized memory items is to be GENERALIZABLE and TRANSFERABLE — each insight must be useful not just for this problem, but for a broad class of similar problems. Write each memory as advice that would help someone who has never seen this specific problem.

## Important notes
- You must first think about why certain attempts succeeded and others failed, then summarize the insights.
- You can extract multiple memory items from the comparison. Each should capture a distinct, independent insight.
- Prioritize GENERAL META-REASONING techniques over object-level hints. Favor principles about representation choice, constraint formalization, invariant selection, validation, decomposition, debugging, or algorithm selection.
- Convert failure observations into POSITIVE reusable guidance. Do not write memories that are just restatements of one wrong step.
- Do NOT mention specific values, answers, variable names, or problem-specific details. Every memory item must stand on its own as a general problem-solving principle or technique that transfers across different problems in the same domain.
- Avoid narrow one-off tricks unless they clearly transfer to a recurring class of problems. Fewer high-quality memories are better than many repetitive ones.
- Emphasize high-level strategies that would still make sense on a different problem in the same domain.

## Examples of good meta-reasoning memories
- Title: Constraint-First Reformulation
  Description: Turn informal requirements into explicit constraints before solving.
  Content: Rewrite vague conditions as equations, inequalities, parity rules, or state constraints. Use those formal constraints to guide the solution and reject invalid branches early.
- Title: Validate Risky Transformations
  Description: Check any step that can introduce invalid candidates against the original problem.
  Content: Squaring, dividing, relaxing constraints, or compressing state can create artifacts that look valid only in the transformed setting. Add a direct verification pass against the original conditions before committing to an answer.
- Title: Model the Smallest Faithful State
  Description: Reduce the problem to the minimal state that still determines future decisions.
  Content: If only parity, remainder class, last element, or turn matters, build the reasoning or DP around that reduced state instead of the full object. This shrinks search and makes transitions easier to verify.

### Problem
You are given a binary array nums.
A subarray of an array is good if it contains exactly one element with the value 1.
Return an integer denoting the number of ways to split the array nums into good subarrays. As the number may be too large, return it modulo 10^9 + 7.
A subarray is a contiguous non-empty sequence of elements within an array.
 
Example 1:

Input: nums = [0,1,0,0,1]
Output: 3
Explanation: There are 3 ways to split nums into good subarrays:
- [0,1] [0,0,1]
- [0,1,0] [0,1]
- [0,1,0,0] [1]

Example 2:

Input: nums = [0,1,0]
Output: 1
Explanation: There is 1 way to split nums into good subarrays:
- [0,1,0]

 
Constraints:

1 <= nums.length <= 10^5
0 <= nums[i] <= 1

### Solutions
#### Correct
Attempt 1
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        positions = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(positions)

        # If there are no ones, it's impossible to have any good subarray (requires exactly one 1).
        if k == 0:
            return 0

        # Each good subarray must contain exactly one 1, so we must split into k parts.
        # For consecutive ones at positions a and b, let gap = b - a - 1 (zeros between them).
        # We must place some suffix of those zeros with the left subarray and the rest with the right.
        # That gives (gap + 1) choices per adjacent pair of ones.
        ans = 1
        for i in range(k - 1):
            gap = positions[i + 1] - positions[i] - 1
            ans = (ans * (gap + 1)) % MOD

        # Zeros before the first 1 and after the last 1 are forced to belong to their respective nearest 1 subarray.
        return ans
```

Attempt 2
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)
        if k == 0:
            return 0  # cannot form good subarrays (each good subarray must contain exactly one 1)

        # dp over groups of ones:
        # Split positions can be placed only in zeros between consecutive ones.
        # For each gap (one-to-one), if there are z zeros between them, then each split
        # boundary inside that gap has (z choices). Additionally, all zeros can be distributed
        # in a way that guarantees the adjacent ones end up in different subarrays.
        # In fact, number of valid split ways equals product over gaps of (z + 1),
        # where z is number of zeros between consecutive ones.
        #
        # Zeros at the ends (before first 1 and after last 1) are forced into the only
        # subarrays that contain those boundary ones.
        ans = 1
        for i in range(1, k):
            left = ones[i - 1]
            right = ones[i]
            z = right - left - 1  # zeros between these two ones
            ans = (ans * (z + 1)) % MOD

        return ans
```

Attempt 3
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        # Collect indices of all ones
        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)

        # If no ones, impossible because each good subarray must contain exactly one '1'
        if k == 0:
            return 0

        # If exactly one 1, the whole array must be taken as the single good subarray
        if k == 1:
            return 1

        # For consecutive ones at positions p=ones[i], q=ones[i+1],
        # we can choose where the split boundary falls inside the gap [p+1, q-1].
        # That count equals number of zeros between them, plus 1.
        ans = 1
        for i in range(k - 1):
            gap_zeros = ones[i + 1] - ones[i] - 1
            ans = (ans * (gap_zeros + 1)) % MOD

        return ans
```

Attempt 4
```python
from typing import List

MOD = 10**9 + 7

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)
        if k == 0:
            return 0
        if k == 1:
            # Whole array is the only good subarray split
            return 1

        ans = 1
        # For every gap between consecutive ones: indices (ones[j], ones[j+1])
        # Let d be the number of zeros between them.
        # We need choose a cut position after the earlier one within the zeros,
        # ensuring each segment contains exactly one '1'.
        # Number of choices = zeros_between + 1 = ones[j+1] - ones[j]
        for j in range(k - 1):
            zeros_between = ones[j+1] - ones[j] - 1
            ans = (ans * (zeros_between + 1)) % MOD
        return ans
```

from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7
        n = len(nums)

        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)

        # Every good subarray must contain exactly one '1',
        # so if there are 0 or more than 0 ones constraints make impossible.
        if k == 0:
            return 0  # cannot form good subarray (non-empty) with exactly one 1

        # If we split into k good subarrays, between each consecutive pair of ones,
        # we may place the boundary somewhere in the zeros-run between them.
        ans = 1
        for j in range(k - 1):
            gap = ones[j + 1] - ones[j] - 1  # number of zeros between two ones
            ans = (ans * (gap + 1)) % MOD

        return ans
```

Attempt 6
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        # Total count of ways:
        # Let positions of ones be at indices p0 < p1 < ... < p{k-1}.
        # Each segment must contain exactly one '1'.
        # Zeros after the first one and before the next one must be distributed
        # between neighboring segments: if there are (d) zeros between ones,
        # there are (d+1) ways for the boundary.
        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)
        if k == 0:
            return 0  # cannot split into non-empty good subarrays

        ans = 1
        for i in range(k - 1):
            zeros_between = ones[i + 1] - ones[i] - 1
            ans = (ans * (zeros_between + 1)) % MOD
        return ans
```

Attempt 7
```python
from typing import List

MOD = 10**9 + 7

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        # Find positions of all 1s
        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)
        
        # If there are 0 ones, it's impossible to have any good subarray
        # (but constraints don't specify; still handle safely)
        if k == 0:
            return 0
        
        # If there's only one 1, the whole array must be exactly one good subarray
        # because any split would create a subarray without exactly one '1'.
        if k == 1:
            return 1
        
        # Between consecutive 1s, count zeros separating them.
        # Let zeros[i] be number of zeros between ones[i] and ones[i+1].
        ways = 1
        for i in range(k - 1):
            gap = ones[i + 1] - ones[i] - 1  # number of zeros between these ones
            # We must decide where to cut: after the i-th 1's subarray,
            # so choose an index among gap zeros plus 1 cut options.
            ways = (ways * (gap + 1)) % MOD
        
        return ways
```

Attempt 8
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        # Positions of ones
        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)

        # If no 1, can't make any non-empty subarray with exactly one 1
        if k == 0:
            return 0

        # If exactly one 1, the whole array must be the single good subarray
        # (and it will contain exactly that one 1). Only 1 split.
        if k == 1:
            return 1

        # Let gaps[i] be the number of zeros between ones[i] and ones[i+1]
        # For each gap, we distribute some trailing zeros of the left part
        # to the left subarray so that the right subarray still contains exactly
        # one 1. That gives (gap + 1) choices per internal boundary.
        ans = 1
        for i in range(k - 1):
            gap_zeros = ones[i + 1] - ones[i] - 1
            ans = (ans * (gap_zeros + 1)) % MOD

        return ans
```

Attempt 9
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        # Count ones and positions of ones
        ones = [i for i, v in enumerate(nums) if v == 1]
        k = len(ones)
        if k == 0:
            return 0  # cannot make any good subarray (needs exactly one '1' each)

        # For each gap of zeros between consecutive ones, compute choices:
        # Suppose between ones[i] and ones[i+1] there are z zeros.
        # Split boundary must be placed at one of these z+1 cut positions
        # to ensure each side has exactly one 1.
        ans = 1
        for i in range(k - 1):
            left_one = ones[i]
            right_one = ones[i + 1]
            z = right_one - left_one - 1  # number of zeros between the two ones
            ans = (ans * (z + 1)) % MOD

        # Zeros before the first 1 and after the last 1 are forced:
        # left part must end at first 1, right part must start at last 1.
        return ans
```

#### Partially Correct
Attempt 1
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        # Collect indices of all 1s
        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)

        # If no 1 or only one 1: whole array must be the only good subarray
        # (subarray must contain exactly one '1' and must cover the array).
        if k <= 1:
            return 1

        # For two consecutive 1s at positions ones[j-1], ones[j],
        # the subarray containing ones[j-1] ends somewhere in (ones[j-1], ones[j])
        # but must not include ones[j]. So choices are the number of zeros between
        # them plus 1 (cut positions between them).
        ans = 1
        for j in range(1, k):
            gap = ones[j] - ones[j - 1]  # distance between 1s
            # Between them there are gap-1 elements, all zeros.
            # Number of cut choices equals (gap) because start after previous cut,
            # equivalently choose end position for left subarray among those gap elements.
            ans = (ans * gap) % MOD

        return ans
```

### Grouped Solutions

#### Tier: `full`

<details>
<summary>full attempt 1</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        positions = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(positions)

        # If there are no ones, it's impossible to have any good subarray (requires exactly one 1).
        if k == 0:
            return 0

        # Each good subarray must contain exactly one 1, so we must split into k parts.
        # For consecutive ones at positions a and b, let gap = b - a - 1 (zeros between them).
        # We must place some suffix of those zeros with the left subarray and the rest with the right.
        # That gives (gap + 1) choices per adjacent pair of ones.
        ans = 1
        for i in range(k - 1):
            gap = positions[i + 1] - positions[i] - 1
            ans = (ans * (gap + 1)) % MOD

        # Zeros before the first 1 and after the last 1 are forced to belong to their respective nearest 1 subarray.
        return ans
```
```

</details>

<summary>full attempt 2</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)
        if k == 0:
            return 0  # cannot form good subarrays (each good subarray must contain exactly one 1)

        # dp over groups of ones:
        # Split positions can be placed only in zeros between consecutive ones.
        # For each gap (one-to-one), if there are z zeros between them, then each split
        # boundary inside that gap has (z choices). Additionally, all zeros can be distributed
        # in a way that guarantees the adjacent ones end up in different subarrays.
        # In fact, number of valid split ways equals product over gaps of (z + 1),
        # where z is number of zeros between consecutive ones.
        #
        # Zeros at the ends (before first 1 and after last 1) are forced into the only
        # subarrays that contain those boundary ones.
        ans = 1
        for i in range(1, k):
            left = ones[i - 1]
            right = ones[i]
            z = right - left - 1  # zeros between these two ones
            ans = (ans * (z + 1)) % MOD

        return ans
```

```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        # Collect indices of all 1s
        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)

        # If no 1 or only one 1: whole array must be the only good subarray
        # (subarray must contain exactly one '1' and must cover the array).
        if k <= 1:
            return 1

        # For two consecutive 1s at positions ones[j-1], ones[j],
        # the subarray containing ones[j-1] ends somewhere in (ones[j-1], ones[j])
        # but must not include ones[j]. So choices are the number of zeros between
        # them plus 1 (cut positions between them).
        ans = 1
        for j in range(1, k):
            gap = ones[j] - ones[j - 1]  # distance between 1s
            # Between them there are gap-1 elements, all zeros.
            # Number of cut choices equals (gap) because start after previous cut,
            # equivalently choose end position for left subarray among those gap elements.
            ans = (ans * gap) % MOD

        return ans

### Parsed Memory Items

1. Constraint-Driven Boundary Counting
Description: Reformulate the split rule into explicit constraints on where each boundary may lie, then count boundary choices per gap.
<details>
<summary>Content 1</summary>

```text
Identify the minimal structural feature that each segment must contain (e.g., exactly one marked element) and translate “good segment” into hard constraints on boundary placement. Once boundaries are constrained to lie within disjoint regions (typically between consecutive marked elements), the total number of splits often factors as a product of independent choices per region.
```

</details>

<details>
<summary>Reasoning 1</summary>

```text
All successful attempts converge to the same core combinatorial model: every valid split corresponds to choosing, independently for each internal boundary between consecutive “special” elements, how many of the separating neutral elements go to the left vs. right segment. The partially correct attempt fails because it miscounts the number of allowable cut positions for an internal boundary (using an off-by-one/misaligned distance measure), which shows up as returning a value that is systematically too small by a factor related to the correct “gap+1” choice count. The correctness also depends on handling edge cases of “no special elements” vs “exactly one special element” consistently: either no split is possible or the entire array must be a single segment.
```

</details>

2. Off-by-One Auditing for Cut Positions
Description: When counting cut locations inside a gap, compute choices as the number of discrete boundary positions, not just the gap size.
<details>
<summary>Content 2</summary>

```text
For any gap between consecutive special indices, enumerate what it means to choose a cut (e.g., “after some element in the gap” yields gap+1 possible boundary endpoints including extremes). If a partial solution uses a distance like “difference between indices” directly, validate it by mapping each choice to a unique boundary position to catch systematic off-by-one errors.
```

</details>

<details>
<summary>Reasoning 2</summary>

```text
All successful attempts converge to the same core combinatorial model: every valid split corresponds to choosing, independently for each internal boundary between consecutive “special” elements, how many of the separating neutral elements go to the left vs. right segment. The partially correct attempt fails because it miscounts the number of allowable cut positions for an internal boundary (using an off-by-one/misaligned distance measure), which shows up as returning a value that is systematically too small by a factor related to the correct “gap+1” choice count. The correctness also depends on handling edge cases of “no special elements” vs “exactly one special element” consistently: either no split is possible or the entire array must be a single segment.
```

</details>

3. Factor Independence via Locality of Constraints
Description: Prove that internal decisions affect only neighboring segments; then multiply per-boundary counts.
<details>
<summary>Content 3</summary>

```text
After determining allowed boundary regions, check that moving one boundary does not violate the “exactly one marked element per segment” constraint for non-adjacent segments. This locality lets you treat each internal boundary as independent, turning an exponential split search into a product of small counts.
```

</details>

<details>
<summary>Reasoning 3</summary>

```text
All successful attempts converge to the same core combinatorial model: every valid split corresponds to choosing, independently for each internal boundary between consecutive “special” elements, how many of the separating neutral elements go to the left vs. right segment. The partially correct attempt fails because it miscounts the number of allowable cut positions for an internal boundary (using an off-by-one/misaligned distance measure), which shows up as returning a value that is systematically too small by a factor related to the correct “gap+1” choice count. The correctness also depends on handling edge cases of “no special elements” vs “exactly one special element” consistently: either no split is possible or the entire array must be a single segment.
```

</details>

4. Handle Degenerate Cases with Segment-Existence Logic
Description: Before counting, separately reason about cases where the required segment property cannot be satisfied.
<details>
<summary>Content 4</summary>

```text
If the array contains zero instances of the required marked element, no good segment can exist; if it contains exactly one, only one split is possible (the whole array must be that single good segment). Establish these base cases early so the general product formula doesn’t get applied outside its validity range.
```

</details>

<details>
<summary>Reasoning 4</summary>

```text
All successful attempts converge to the same core combinatorial model: every valid split corresponds to choosing, independently for each internal boundary between consecutive “special” elements, how many of the separating neutral elements go to the left vs. right segment. The partially correct attempt fails because it miscounts the number of allowable cut positions for an internal boundary (using an off-by-one/misaligned distance measure), which shows up as returning a value that is systematically too small by a factor related to the correct “gap+1” choice count. The correctness also depends on handling edge cases of “no special elements” vs “exactly one special element” consistently: either no split is possible or the entire array must be a single segment.
```

</details>

<title>Handle Degenerate Cases with Segment-Existence Logic</title>
<description>Before counting, separately reason about cases where the required segment property cannot be satisfied.</description>
<content>If the array contains zero instances of the required marked element, no good segment can exist; if it contains exactly one, only one split is possible (the whole array must be that single good segment). Establish these base cases early so the general product formula doesn’t get applied outside its validity range.</content>
</memory>
</memories>
```

</details>

## Evaluation

- Baseline pass@1: `0.9000`
- Augmented pass@1: `1.0000`
- Improvement: `+0.1000`

### Baseline

### System Prompt

```text
You are an expert Python programmer. You will be given a question (problem specification) and will generate a correct Python program that matches the specification and passes all tests.
```

### User Prompt

```text
### Question:
You are given a binary array nums.
A subarray of an array is good if it contains exactly one element with the value 1.
Return an integer denoting the number of ways to split the array nums into good subarrays. As the number may be too large, return it modulo 10^9 + 7.
A subarray is a contiguous non-empty sequence of elements within an array.
 
Example 1:

Input: nums = [0,1,0,0,1]
Output: 3
Explanation: There are 3 ways to split nums into good subarrays:
- [0,1] [0,0,1]
- [0,1,0] [0,1]
- [0,1,0,0] [1]

Example 2:

Input: nums = [0,1,0]
Output: 1
Explanation: There is 1 way to split nums into good subarrays:
- [0,1,0]

 
Constraints:

1 <= nums.length <= 10^5
0 <= nums[i] <= 1

### Format: You will use the following starter code to write the solution to the problem and enclose your code within delimiters.
```python
class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        
```

### Answer: (use the provided format with backticks)
```

#### Outputs

<details>
<summary>Baseline generation 1</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)

        # If no or only one 1, it's impossible to split into good subarrays
        # because each good subarray must contain exactly one '1',
        # so every subarray would have to contain a '1' -> requires at least two?
        # Actually for k==0: impossible (subarrays can't have exactly one '1').
        # For k==1: the whole array is the only possible split (if array contains only that one '1').
        if k == 0:
            return 0
        if k == 1:
            return 1

        # Let gaps be the number of zeros between consecutive ones
        # For each boundary between one i and i+1, we can place the split
        # somewhere in the overlap: specifically choices = gap + 1.
        ans = 1
        for a, b in zip(ones, ones[1:]):
            gap = b - a - 1  # zeros between
            ans = (ans * (gap + 1)) % MOD

        return ans
```
```

</details>

<details>
<summary>Baseline generation 2</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)

        # If there is no '1', no non-empty subarray can have exactly one 1.
        if k == 0:
            return 0

        # Each good subarray must contain exactly one '1',
        # so we must split so that boundaries are placed between groups of zeros
        # around consecutive ones.
        ans = 1

        # For each gap between consecutive ones, zeros can be distributed:
        # Suppose positions are ... 1 at ones[j], 1 at ones[j+1].
        # Let gap = ones[j+1] - ones[j] - 1 (number of zeros between them).
        # We can choose how many trailing zeros belong to the left subarray;
        # the split point can be after any of the first 'gap' zeros, giving (gap + 1) choices.
        # Additionally, for boundary handling at ends:
        # the first subarray must include all zeros before first 1,
        # and the last subarray must include all zeros after last 1,
        # which impose no choices.
        for j in range(k - 1):
            gap = ones[j + 1] - ones[j] - 1
            ans = (ans * (gap + 1)) % MOD

        return ans
```
```

</details>

<details>
<summary>Baseline generation 3</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7
        n = len(nums)

        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)
        # Each good subarray must contain exactly one 1.
        # So if there are no 1s, impossible (since good subarray must contain exactly one 1).
        if k == 0:
            return 0
        # If there is only one 1, the only valid split is the whole array.
        if k == 1:
            return 1

        # For each adjacent pair of 1s, distribute zeros between them:
        # Suppose positions are ones[j], ones[j+1] with d = ones[j+1] - ones[j].
        # Then there are (d-1) elements between them, all zeros (since positions of ones are fixed).
        # Let zeros_between = d - 1.
        # We may split after the t-th zero for t in [0..zeros_between], giving (zeros_between + 1) choices.
        ans = 1
        for j in range(k - 1):
            zeros_between = ones[j + 1] - ones[j] - 1
            ans = (ans * (zeros_between + 1)) % MOD

        return ans
```
```

</details>

<details>
<summary>Baseline generation 4</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7
        n = len(nums)

        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        if not ones:
            return 0  # can't form good subarrays without a '1'

        k = len(ones)  # number of 1s
        ans = 1
        for j in range(1, k):
            # number of zeros between consecutive ones (there may be split choices there)
            gap = ones[j] - ones[j - 1] - 1
            ans = (ans * (gap + 1)) % MOD

        return ans
```
```

<summary>Baseline generation 5</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

MOD = 10**9 + 7

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        # Positions of ones
        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)
        if k == 0:
            return 0  # no way to make every subarray contain exactly one 1

        # For each gap between consecutive ones:
        # Let len_g be number of zeros strictly between them.
        # We can place a split anywhere in that gap so that each side gets exactly one '1':
        # number of choices = len_g + 1.
        ans = 1
        for idx in range(k - 1):
            gap_len = ones[idx + 1] - ones[idx] - 1  # zeros between consecutive ones
            ans = (ans * (gap_len + 1)) % MOD

        return ans
```

<summary>Baseline generation 6</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        n = len(nums)
        ones = [i for i, v in enumerate(nums) if v == 1]
        k = len(ones)

        # If there are no 1s, impossible to have a good subarray (needs exactly one '1')
        if k == 0:
            return 0

        # Number of ways to choose split points such that each segment contains exactly one '1'
        # Let z_i be zeros count between the i-th and (i+1)-th 1.
        # Then for internal boundaries, there are (z_i + 1) choices.
        ans = 1
        for i in range(k - 1):
            gap = ones[i + 1] - ones[i] - 1  # zeros between these two ones
            ans = (ans * (gap + 1)) % MOD

        return ans
```

<summary>Baseline generation 7</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        # Positions of ones; each good subarray must contain exactly one '1'
        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)

        # If there are 0 or >0 ones, number of subarray splits:
        # - If k == 0: impossible to have a subarray with exactly one 1 (but constraints likely imply at least one 1)
        #   We'll return 0.
        if k == 0:
            return 0

        # A split is possible only if k > 0; for each adjacent pair of ones,
        # choose a cut position between them. Zeros between two ones form flexible boundaries.
        # Let gap = ones[i+1] - ones[i] - 1 zeros between them.
        # For internal ones, the subarray ending after ones[i] must include some zeros and stop before next one,
        # and similarly next subarray starts after previous one includes the remaining zeros.
        # The number of ways contributed by each internal gap is (gap + 1).
        ans = 1
        for i in range(k - 1):
            gap = ones[i + 1] - ones[i] - 1
            ans = (ans * (gap + 1)) % MOD

        return ans
```
```

</details>

<details>
<summary>Baseline generation 8</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7
        n = len(nums)
        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        
        # If there is no '1', impossible to make any good (each good subarray needs exactly one 1)
        if not ones:
            return 0
        
        # Each good subarray must contain exactly one '1', so number of subarrays == number of ones.
        m = len(ones)
        
        # Count zeros between consecutive ones.
        # Example: 1 ... 0s ... 1  => there are gap zeros = zeros between these two ones.
        # For splitting: each gap of zeros gives (gap_zeros + 1) choices on how to distribute
        # between the left subarray and the right subarray.
        ans = 1
        for k in range(m - 1):
            gap = ones[k + 1] - ones[k] - 1  # zeros between ones[k] and ones[k+1]
            ans = (ans * (gap + 1)) % MOD
        
        # Zeros before the first '1' must belong to the first subarray, and zeros after the last '1'
        # must belong to the last subarray => no extra multiplicative choices.
        return ans
```
```

</details>

<details>
<summary>Baseline generation 9</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7
        
        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)
        if k <= 1:
            return 1  # either already good (k=1) or no valid split (k=0 won't happen per constraints usage)
        
        # For split into good subarrays, we must cut between ones so each segment has exactly one '1'.
        # Let gaps = number of zeros between consecutive ones.
        # If gap_j = ones[j] - ones[j-1] - 1, then boundary between those segments can be chosen in:
        # (gap_j + 1) ways (place cut after any zero from the left part, or right at the '1').
        ans = 1
        for j in range(1, k):
            gap = ones[j] - ones[j - 1] - 1
            ans = (ans * (gap + 1)) % MOD
        return ans
```
```

</details>

<details>
<summary>Baseline generation 10</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        n = len(nums)
        pos = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(pos)

        # If there are no 1s (can't form any good subarray: each good subarray needs exactly one 1)
        if k == 0:
            return 0

        # If there is exactly one 1, the whole array must be one good subarray
        # (Since subarrays must cover all elements contiguously.)
        if k == 1:
            return 1

        # For i-th 1, let:
        # - left gap g_i = number of zeros between pos[i-1] and pos[i] (excluding the ones themselves)
        # - right extra zeros after last 1 are determined by the split at boundaries
        #
        # Each internal boundary between ones occurs within the zeros between them.
        # If between pos[i] and pos[i+1] there are g zeros, then boundary can be placed in (g + 1) ways.
        # Additionally, the zeros before the first 1 must belong to the subarray containing the first 1
        # and zeros after the last 1 must belong to the subarray containing the last 1 (forced).
        ans = 1
        for i in range(k - 1):
            gap = pos[i + 1] - pos[i] - 1  # zeros between consecutive ones
            ans = (ans * (gap + 1)) % MOD

        return ans
```
```

### Augmented

### System Prompt

```text
You are an expert Python programmer. You will be given a question (problem specification) and will generate a correct Python program that matches the specification and passes all tests.
```

### User Prompt

```text
Before solving, consider these reusable insights from prior analysis:

  1. Constraint-Driven Boundary Counting: Reformulate the split rule into explicit constraints on where each boundary may lie, then count boundary choices per gap.
     Identify the minimal structural feature that each segment must contain (e.g., exactly one marked element) and translate “good segment” into hard constraints on boundary placement. Once boundaries are constrained to lie within disjoint regions (typically between consecutive marked elements), the total number of splits often factors as a product of independent choices per region.
  2. Off-by-One Auditing for Cut Positions: When counting cut locations inside a gap, compute choices as the number of discrete boundary positions, not just the gap size.
     For any gap between consecutive special indices, enumerate what it means to choose a cut (e.g., “after some element in the gap” yields gap+1 possible boundary endpoints including extremes). If a partial solution uses a distance like “difference between indices” directly, validate it by mapping each choice to a unique boundary position to catch systematic off-by-one errors.
  3. Factor Independence via Locality of Constraints: Prove that internal decisions affect only neighboring segments; then multiply per-boundary counts.
     After determining allowed boundary regions, check that moving one boundary does not violate the “exactly one marked element per segment” constraint for non-adjacent segments. This locality lets you treat each internal boundary as independent, turning an exponential split search into a product of small counts.
  4. Handle Degenerate Cases with Segment-Existence Logic: Before counting, separately reason about cases where the required segment property cannot be satisfied.
     If the array contains zero instances of the required marked element, no good segment can exist; if it contains exactly one, only one split is possible (the whole array must be that single good segment). Establish these base cases early so the general product formula doesn’t get applied outside its validity range.

Apply these insights to the problem below.

### Question:
You are given a binary array nums.
A subarray of an array is good if it contains exactly one element with the value 1.
Return an integer denoting the number of ways to split the array nums into good subarrays. As the number may be too large, return it modulo 10^9 + 7.
A subarray is a contiguous non-empty sequence of elements within an array.
 
Example 1:

Input: nums = [0,1,0,0,1]
Output: 3
Explanation: There are 3 ways to split nums into good subarrays:
- [0,1] [0,0,1]
- [0,1,0] [0,1]
- [0,1,0,0] [1]

Example 2:

Input: nums = [0,1,0]
Output: 1
Explanation: There is 1 way to split nums into good subarrays:
- [0,1,0]

 
Constraints:

1 <= nums.length <= 10^5
0 <= nums[i] <= 1

### Format: You will use the following starter code to write the solution to the problem and enclose your code within delimiters.
```python
class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        
```

### Answer: (use the provided format with backticks)
```

#### Outputs

<details>
<summary>Augmented generation 1</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

MOD = 10**9 + 7

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        n = len(nums)
        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        m = len(ones)

        # Degenerate cases
        if m == 0:
            return 0
        if m == 1:
            return 1  # only one way: the whole array is the single good subarray

        # For each adjacent pair of ones, the boundary between their segments
        # must be placed somewhere strictly between the two ones.
        # If ones are at positions a and b (a < b), there are (b - a) choices
        # for the boundary index k such that:
        #   left segment ends at k in [a, b-1]
        #   right segment starts at k+1 in [a+1, b]
        # => number of possible k is (b - a).
        ans = 1
        for i in range(1, m):
            ans = (ans * (ones[i] - ones[i - 1])) % MOD
        return ans
```
```

</details>

<summary>Augmented generation 2</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

MOD = 10**9 + 7

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)

        if k == 0:
            return 0
        if k == 1:
            return 1

        # Between consecutive ones at positions a=ones[i], b=ones[i+1]:
        # There are (b-a-1) zeros. We must place exactly one cut between them so that
        # each segment contains exactly one '1'. The boundary can be at any position
        # inside the gap in a way that assigns some suffix of the left gap to the left
        # segment and the rest to the right segment.
        #
        # Number of valid boundary positions = (b - a)
        # (because we can choose the first index of the right segment anywhere from a+1..b)
        ans = 1
        for i in range(k - 1):
            ans = (ans * (ones[i + 1] - ones[i])) % MOD
        return ans
```
```

<summary>Augmented generation 3</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

MOD = 10**9 + 7

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)

        if k == 0:
            return 0
        if k == 1:
            return 1

        ans = 1
        # Between consecutive ones at positions ones[j], ones[j+1]
        # there are d = ones[j+1] - ones[j] - 1 zeros.
        # The cut between the two segments can be placed after any position
        # from ones[j] through ones[j+1]-1 => (d+1) choices.
        for j in range(k - 1):
            gap = ones[j + 1] - ones[j] - 1
            ans = (ans * (gap + 1)) % MOD

        return ans
```
```

<summary>Augmented generation 4</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

MOD = 10**9 + 7

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)

        # Degenerate cases
        if k == 0:
            return 0
        if k == 1:
            return 1  # whole array must be one good subarray

        # For each adjacent pair of 1s at positions a < b:
        # boundary between the subarrays that contain exactly one 1
        # must lie between them, and can be at any position from a+1 .. b-1
        # plus the option of cutting right after the left 1 (i.e., boundary at a+1),
        # and right before the right 1 (boundary at b), which is exactly (b - a - 1) + 1
        # => (b - a) choices.
        ans = 1
        for i in range(k - 1):
            gap_choices = ones[i + 1] - ones[i]  # number of valid boundary positions
            ans = (ans * gap_choices) % MOD

        return ans
```
```

```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)

        # Each good subarray must contain exactly one '1'.
        if k == 0:
            return 0
        if k == 1:
            return 1

        # For each gap between consecutive 1s:
        # let a = ones[j], b = ones[j+1]
        # the boundary between the two segments must be placed so that:
        # - left segment includes exactly the 1 at a
        # - right segment includes exactly the 1 at b
        # Therefore the cut must be after some index in (a, b),
        # i.e., between a+1..b inclusive as end positions of the left segment.
        # Count = (b - a) possibilities.
        ans = 1
        for j in range(k - 1):
            ans = (ans * (ones[j + 1] - ones[j])) % MOD

        return ans
```
```

<summary>Augmented generation 6</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)

        if k == 0:
            return 0
        if k == 1:
            return 1  # whole array must be the only good subarray

        # For each adjacent pair of ones at positions ones[i-1], ones[i],
        # the boundary between their corresponding good subarrays must lie
        # somewhere between them.
        # Specifically, the i-th boundary must be chosen at one of (gap size) + 1
        # discrete cut positions between indices:
        # cut can be after any element in (ones[i-1], ones[i]) inclusive of extremes,
        # yielding: (ones[i] - ones[i-1]) possibilities for cut end within that gap,
        # but total discrete boundary positions = ones[i] - ones[i-1].
        #
        # More concretely: if left segment contains ones[i-1] and right contains ones[i],
        # then left segment must end at some position e with ones[i-1] <= e < ones[i].
        # There are (ones[i] - ones[i-1]) choices.
        ans = 1
        for i in range(1, k):
            ans = (ans * (ones[i] - ones[i - 1])) % MOD

        return ans
```
```

</details>

<summary>Augmented generation 7</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7

        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)

        if k == 0:
            return 0
        if k == 1:
            return 1  # the whole array must be the only good subarray

        # There are k good subarrays, each containing exactly one "1".
        # Boundaries must be placed between ones: between ones[j] and ones[j+1],
        # the split boundary must fall in that gap (possibly right next to a 1),
        # giving (ones[j+1] - ones[j]) choices.
        ans = 1
        for j in range(k - 1):
            ans = (ans * (ones[j + 1] - ones[j])) % MOD
        return ans
```
```

<summary>Augmented generation 8</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        MOD = 10**9 + 7
        ones = [i for i, v in enumerate(nums) if v == 1]
        k = len(ones)

        if k == 0:
            return 0
        if k == 1:
            # The entire array must be the only good subarray (exactly one 1).
            return 1

        # Split must create k good segments, each containing exactly one 1.
        # Between consecutive ones, the boundary can be placed in any position that
        # keeps earlier segment ending before the later segment's 1 and later segment
        # starting after the earlier segment's boundary.
        #
        # For two consecutive ones at positions a < b, the boundary between their segments
        # can be after any element in [a, b-1], giving (b-a) choices.
        ans = 1
        for i in range(k - 1):
            ans = (ans * (ones[i + 1] - ones[i])) % MOD
        return ans
```
```

<summary>Augmented generation 9</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

MOD = 10**9 + 7

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)

        if k == 0:
            return 0
        if k == 1:
            # Whole array must be exactly one good subarray
            return 1

        # For each adjacent pair of ones, the boundary must be placed between them
        # so that each segment contains exactly one "1".
        # The boundary can be after any position from (ones[j] + 1) ... (ones[j+1] - 1),
        # inclusive ends in terms of boundary positions => (ones[j+1] - ones[j]) choices.
        ans = 1
        for j in range(k - 1):
            gap = ones[j + 1] - ones[j]
            ans = (ans * gap) % MOD

        return ans
```
```

<summary>Augmented generation 10</summary>

```text
```python
from typing import List

MOD = 10**9 + 7

class Solution:
    def numberOfGoodSubarraySplits(self, nums: List[int]) -> int:
        ones = [i for i, x in enumerate(nums) if x == 1]
        k = len(ones)

        # Each good subarray must contain exactly one '1'.
        if k == 0:
            return 0
        if k == 1:
            return 1  # the whole array is the only good subarray

        ans = 1

        # For each gap between consecutive ones at positions ones[i-1] and ones[i]:
        # Let a = ones[i-1], b = ones[i], with a < b.
        # We must place exactly one boundary between these two ones somewhere in (a, b).
        # Choices correspond to choosing the last element of the left segment,
        # which can be any index from a to b-1 inclusive => (b-a) choices.
        for i in range(1, k):
            ans = (ans * (ones[i] - ones[i - 1])) % MOD

        return ans
```
```
